  <title>A High Quality Draft Consensus Sequence of the Genome of a Heterozygous Grapevine Variety</title>

  <link rel="canonical" href="https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371/journal.pone.0001326" />
  <meta name="description" content="BackgroundWorldwide, grapes and their derived products have a large market. The cultivated grape species Vitis vinifera has potential to become a model for fruit trees genetics. Like many plant species, it is highly heterozygous, which is an additional challenge to modern whole genome shotgun sequencing. In this paper a high quality draft genome sequence of a cultivated clone of V. vinifera Pinot Noir is presented.Principal FindingsWe estimate the genome size of V. vinifera to be 504.6 Mb. Genomic sequences corresponding to 477.1 Mb were assembled in 2,093 metacontigs and 435.1 Mb were anchored to the 19 linkage groups (LGs). The number of predicted genes is 29,585, of which 96.1% were assigned to LGs. This assembly of the grape genome provides candidate genes implicated in traits relevant to grapevine cultivation, such as those influencing wine quality, via secondary metabolites, and those connected with the extreme susceptibility of grape to pathogens. Single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) distribution was consistent with a diffuse haplotype structure across the genome. Of around 2,000,000 SNPs, 1,751,176 were mapped to chromosomes and one or more of them were identified in 86.7% of anchored genes. The relative age of grape duplicated genes was estimated and this made possible to reveal a relatively recent Vitis-specific large scale duplication event concerning at least 10 chromosomes (duplication not reported before).ConclusionsSanger shotgun sequencing and highly efficient sequencing by synthesis (SBS), together with dedicated assembly programs, resolved a complex heterozygous genome. A consensus sequence of the genome and a set of mapped marker loci were generated. Homologous chromosomes of Pinot Noir differ by 11.2% of their DNA (hemizygous DNA plus chromosomal gaps). SNP markers are offered as a tool with the potential of introducing a new era in the molecular breeding of grape." />

  <meta name="citation_abstract" content="BackgroundWorldwide, grapes and their derived products have a large market. The cultivated grape species Vitis vinifera has potential to become a model for fruit trees genetics. Like many plant species, it is highly heterozygous, which is an additional challenge to modern whole genome shotgun sequencing. In this paper a high quality draft genome sequence of a cultivated clone of V. vinifera Pinot Noir is presented.Principal FindingsWe estimate the genome size of V. vinifera to be 504.6 Mb. Genomic sequences corresponding to 477.1 Mb were assembled in 2,093 metacontigs and 435.1 Mb were anchored to the 19 linkage groups (LGs). The number of predicted genes is 29,585, of which 96.1% were assigned to LGs. This assembly of the grape genome provides candidate genes implicated in traits relevant to grapevine cultivation, such as those influencing wine quality, via secondary metabolites, and those connected with the extreme susceptibility of grape to pathogens. Single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) distribution was consistent with a diffuse haplotype structure across the genome. Of around 2,000,000 SNPs, 1,751,176 were mapped to chromosomes and one or more of them were identified in 86.7% of anchored genes. The relative age of grape duplicated genes was estimated and this made possible to reveal a relatively recent Vitis-specific large scale duplication event concerning at least 10 chromosomes (duplication not reported before).ConclusionsSanger shotgun sequencing and highly efficient sequencing by synthesis (SBS), together with dedicated assembly programs, resolved a complex heterozygous genome. A consensus sequence of the genome and a set of mapped marker loci were generated. Homologous chromosomes of Pinot Noir differ by 11.2% of their DNA (hemizygous DNA plus chromosomal gaps). SNP markers are offered as a tool with the potential of introducing a new era in the molecular breeding of grape.">

        <div class="article-text" id="artText">
          



<div class="abstract toc-section abstract-type-"><a id="abstract0" name="abstract0" data-toc="abstract0" class="link-target" title="Abstract"></a><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="abstract-content">
<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="" name="" class="link-target" title="Background"></a>
<h3>Background</h3>
<a id="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract1.sec1.p1" name="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract1.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Worldwide, grapes and their derived products have a large market. The cultivated grape species <em>Vitis vinifera</em> has potential to become a model for fruit trees genetics. Like many plant species, it is highly heterozygous, which is an additional challenge to modern whole genome shotgun sequencing. In this paper a high quality draft genome sequence of a cultivated clone of <em>V. vinifera</em> Pinot Noir is presented.</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="" name="" class="link-target" title="Principal Findings"></a>
<h3>Principal Findings</h3>
<a id="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract1.sec2.p1" name="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract1.sec2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>We estimate the genome size of <em>V. vinifera</em> to be 504.6 Mb. Genomic sequences corresponding to 477.1 Mb were assembled in 2,093 metacontigs and 435.1 Mb were anchored to the 19 linkage groups (LGs). The number of predicted genes is 29,585, of which 96.1% were assigned to LGs. This assembly of the grape genome provides candidate genes implicated in traits relevant to grapevine cultivation, such as those influencing wine quality, via secondary metabolites, and those connected with the extreme susceptibility of grape to pathogens. Single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) distribution was consistent with a diffuse haplotype structure across the genome. Of around 2,000,000 SNPs, 1,751,176 were mapped to chromosomes and one or more of them were identified in 86.7% of anchored genes. The relative age of grape duplicated genes was estimated and this made possible to reveal a relatively recent <em>Vitis</em>-specific large scale duplication event concerning at least 10 chromosomes (duplication not reported before).</p>
</div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="" name="" class="link-target" title="Conclusions"></a>
<h3>Conclusions</h3>
<a id="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract1.sec3.p1" name="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract1.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Sanger shotgun sequencing and highly efficient sequencing by synthesis (SBS), together with dedicated assembly programs, resolved a complex heterozygous genome. A consensus sequence of the genome and a set of mapped marker loci were generated. Homologous chromosomes of Pinot Noir differ by 11.2% of their DNA (hemizygous DNA plus chromosomal gaps). SNP markers are offered as a tool with the potential of introducing a new era in the molecular breeding of grape.</p>
</div>
</div></div>


<div class="articleinfo"><p><strong>Citation: </strong>Velasco R, Zharkikh A, Troggio M, Cartwright DA, Cestaro A, Pruss D, et al.  (2007) A High Quality Draft Consensus Sequence of the Genome of a Heterozygous Grapevine Variety. PLoS ONE 2(12):
           e1326.
        
        https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326</p><p><strong>Academic Editor: </strong>Brian Dilkes, University of California at Davis, Genome Center, United States of America</p><p><strong>Received: </strong>October 5, 2007; <strong>Accepted: </strong>November 21, 2007; <strong>Published: </strong> December 19, 2007</p><p><strong>Copyright: </strong> © 2007 Velasco et al. This is an open-access article distributed under the terms of the Creative Commons Attribution License, which permits unrestricted use, distribution, and reproduction in any medium, provided the original author and source are credited.</p><p><strong>Funding: </strong>This work has been funded by the Province of Trento, Italy. The funders had no role in study design, data collection and analysis, decision to publish, or preparation of the manuscript.</p><p><strong>Competing interests: </strong> The authors have declared that no competing interests exist.</p></div>





<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="s1" name="s1" data-toc="s1" class="link-target" title="Introduction"></a><h2>Introduction</h2><a id="article1.body1.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Grapes (67 million t; <a href="http://faostat.fao.org/site/336/DesktopDefault.aspx">http://faostat.fao.org/site/336/DesktopDefault.aspx</a>) and their derivatives have a large and expanding worldwide market. Grapes can be grown at latitudes from 50°N to 40°S and up to 3,000 meters above sea level, with almost 98% of grape vineyards planted with <em>Vitis vinifera</em> L. ssp. <em>sativa</em> cultivars of Eurasian origin. Ever since the development of wine-making in Iran between 5,440 and 5,000 B.C. <a href="#pone.0001326-McGovern1" class="ref-tip">[1]</a>, wine has been an important component of many cultures. It has been celebrated by the Ecclesiates, by Horace, Goethe, Jefferson and the Nobel laureate J. C. Cela. A traditional icon of the Mediterranean diet <a href="#pone.0001326-Panagiotakos1" class="ref-tip">[2]</a>, the grape has more recently been extensively cultivated in the New World and its cultivation is now moving to Asia. Given grape's content of resveratrol, quercitin and ellagic acid, grape products may contribute to reducing the incidence of cardiovascular and other diseases <a href="#pone.0001326-Burns1" class="ref-tip">[3]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p2" name="article1.body1.sec1.p2" class="link-target"></a><p><em>V. vinifera</em> ssp. s<em>ativa</em>, domesticated from the wild ssp. <em>sylvestris</em> <a href="#pone.0001326-Levadoux1" class="ref-tip">[4]</a>, bears hermaphroditic self-fertilizing flowers. However, outbreeding by means of wind and insect pollination is the norm. As a result, cultivars are highly heterozygous and carry many deleterious recessive mutations <a href="#pone.0001326-Olmo1" class="ref-tip">[5]</a>. Inbreeding depression is severe, so that sterility often ensues from the second or third generation of selfing. All wild <em>Vitis</em> species have 38 chromosomes (n = 19) and most interspecies hybrids are fertile <a href="#pone.0001326-Olmo1" class="ref-tip">[5]</a>. The high chromosome number suggests a paleopolyploid state of the genome <a href="#pone.0001326-Lewis1" class="ref-tip">[6]</a>, an argument recently presented in the frame of a recent partial assembly of the grape genome <a href="#pone.0001326-Jaillon1" class="ref-tip">[7]</a> but still remaining controversial.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p3" name="article1.body1.sec1.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Grape has the potential to become a model organism for fruit trees. The species can be transformed <a href="#pone.0001326-Wang1" class="ref-tip">[8]</a> and micropropagated <em>via</em> somatic embryogenesis <a href="#pone.0001326-Kikkert1" class="ref-tip">[9]</a>. Compared to other perennials, the genome size is relatively small, 475 Mb <a href="#pone.0001326-Lodhi1" class="ref-tip">[10]</a>, similar to rice (<em>Oryza sativa</em>, 430 Mb; <a href="#pone.0001326-Goff1" class="ref-tip">[11]</a>), barrel medic <em>(Medicago truncatula,</em> 500 Mb, <a href="http://medicago.org/">http://medicago.org/</a>) and black cottonwood poplar <em>(Populus trichocarpa</em>, 465 Mb; <a href="#pone.0001326-Tuskan1" class="ref-tip">[12]</a>).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p4" name="article1.body1.sec1.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>In this paper we report a high-quality draft sequence of the grapevine genome. The genome is derived from the Pinot Noir clone ENTAV 115, a variety grown in a range of soils for the production of red and sparkling wines. The sequence provides information on the overall organization, gene content and structural components of the DNA of the 19 LGs of <em>V. vinifera</em>. The Sanger sequencing method was used to generate 6.5X coverage of the genome. This has been integrated with sequence reads generated by a scalable, highly parallel sequencing by synthesis (SBS) method with throughput significantly greater than capillary electrophoresis. The 4.2X coverage provided by SBS was crucial in identifying polymorphic sites and in closing most of the gaps between DNA contigs. This is the first project which utilizes both the longer Sanger and shorter SBS methods to determine the sequence of a large eukaryotic genome.</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2" name="s2" data-toc="s2" class="link-target" title="Results and Discussion"></a><h2>Results and Discussion</h2>
<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2a" name="s2a" class="link-target" title="Sequencing and assembly"></a>
<h3>Sequencing and assembly</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The DNA of <em>V. vinifera</em> was extracted from young shoots and sequenced and assembled using the whole genome shotgun (WGS) method. Two techniques were adopted: the Sanger dye primer sequencing of paired reads <a href="#pone.0001326-Sanger1" class="ref-tip">[13]</a> and 454 (SBS) of unpaired reads <a href="#pone.0001326-Margulies1" class="ref-tip">[14]</a>, which provided 6.5X and 4.2X genome coverage respectively (see <a href="#s3">Materials and Methods</a>).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>In order to develop criteria for assembly, a preliminary experiment was conducted to assess heterozygosity: it was found to correspond to approximately 1 SNP per 0.1 Kb and 1 in/del per 0.45 Kb (see Text S1). The assembly program <a href="#pone.0001326-Goff1" class="ref-tip">[11]</a> was accordingly modified to accept a specified level of mismatches in overlapping sequences (details in <a href="#s3">Materials and Methods</a> and in Text S1). The program also incorporated information on clone size, which ranged from 2 to 130 Kb (Table S1).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>The assembly started with unique sequences and progressively included sequences with a higher degree of repetitiveness. To avoid merging repeats into a single genomic sequence, the overlapping unique sequence contigs were merged if the rate of polymorphism did not exceed 2% and if the resulting sequence coverage of the overlap did not exceed 150% of the average coverage (see Text S1). These criteria were modified so that contigs with many supporting links were merged. In most cases, this procedure produced a correct assembly.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>Applying the procedure to about 6.6 M reads from Sanger sequencing, 90.6% of which represented paired clone ends, 211,374 initial seed contigs of unique sequences were generated. By using long clone links with non-repetitive clone ends, seed contigs were ordered into metacontigs (ordered assembly of contigs, referred to as supercontigs or scaffolds in other publications). After the sequences were merged into 120,000 contigs, data were combined with 4.2 genome-equivalents of SBS data. This helped to identify polymorphic sites and closed 25% of the remaining gaps between contigs. After removal of 10,847 contigs composed only of tandemly repeated sequences and disposal of 7,003 contigs shorter than 1,000 bp, the iterative assembly produced 58,611 contigs (Figure S1 and Table S2) corresponding to 530.9 Mb of genomic DNA. 44,179 of the 58,611 contigs were assembled into 2,093 metacontigs and the remaining 14,432 contigs were singletons. The final assembled sequences are deposited at the EMBL/Genbank/DDBJ databases (accession numbers: AM423240-AM489403, data released 2006-12-19). Metacontig data are available at <a href="http://genomics.research.iasma.it">http://genomics.research.iasma.it</a>. The removed contigs represented mostly centromeric and rRNA gene sequences. Based on their read coverage, their sizes were estimated as 14.5 Mb and 16.3 Mb, respectively.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p5" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>Cultivated <em>V. vinifera</em> is highly heterozygous. As a result, many of the resulting contigs were consensus sequences derived from an alignment of the two haplotypes. The set of Pinot Noir chromosome pairs included a considerable number of haplotype-specific gaps (sequences present in one haplotype but not in the other; on this issue see also the ‘Pinot Noir genome structure and evolution’ section). The total length of the 1,042,174 identified gaps corresponded to 48.9 Mb. In some chromosomal regions, the two alternative haplotypes were too different for the algorithm employed during assembly to combine them into a single contig. Such separated contigs corresponded to the hemizygous DNA (22,061 contigs with the total length of 65.1 Mb). The total size of the genome represented by different homologous chromosomes can be estimated as twice the length of the sequences represented by the two haplotypes merged into a consensus (416.8×2 = 833.6 Mb), plus the sequence length represented by hemizygous DNA and gaps, respectively 65.1 and 48.9 Mb. After including the centromeric and rRNA regions (14.5×2+16.3×2 = 61.6 Mb), the size of the diploid genome was subsequently estimated to be 1,009.2 Mb, which gives an average 504.6 Mb per haploid genome (<a href="#pone-0001326-t001">Table 1</a>).</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0001326-t001" name="pone-0001326-t001"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t001"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t001" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t001"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t001" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t001">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t001">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t001">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t001">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t001">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t001">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Table 1. </span> Number and sizes of assembled sequences in Mb.</div><p class="caption_target"></p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t001">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t001</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p6" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p6" class="link-target"></a><p>A region of 403,443 bp (preliminary experiment; see Text S1) was used to monitor the correctness of the assembly. Thirty four of the 37 contigs which mapped to the preliminary experiment sequence belonged to the metacontig assembled from the full genome sequence and were in the correct order. The remaining three contigs were not included because they contained repetitive clone links. Twenty two of the 36 boundaries between adjacent contigs were overlapping but not aligned due to large heterozygous inserts. The remaining 14 contig pairs corresponded to gaps: nine short gaps between 52 and 354 bp and five gaps larger than 500 bp. The largest gap (2.4 Kb) contained tandem repeats. Most of the gaps were associated to heterozygous inserts of repetitive elements. The total gap size, 8,067 bp, corresponded to about 2% of the region considered.</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2b" name="s2b" class="link-target" title="Metacontig integration into the genetic map"></a>
<h3>Metacontig integration into the genetic map</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The next phase of the assembly involved positioning metacontigs in the genome using a genetic map developed at the Istituto Agrario di San Michele all'Adige (IASMA). Genetic mapping was based on 94 individuals derived from a F<sub>1 </sub>Syrah X Pinot Noir cross where the latter was the pollen donor. The map contained 1,006 markers <a href="#pone.0001326-Troggio1" class="ref-tip">[15]</a>, which were used both to anchor BAC contigs to a physical map (<a href="http://genomics.research.iasma.it">http://genomics.research.iasma.it</a>) and to order metacontigs along linkage groups (LGs).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>A set of 799 additional SNP markers was developed based on polymorphic sites identified in contigs and was used to anchor and orient metacontigs to LGs. This genetic map included 1,767 molecular markers arranged in 19 LGs covering 1,276 cM (Figure S2; <a href="http://genomics.research.iasma.it">http://genomics.research.iasma.it</a>). The SNP-based markers were also helpful in merging the adjacent metacontigs not previously merged because of repetitive or low-quality links between them.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Integration of the DNA sequence and genetic map of LG4 is shown in <a href="#pone-0001326-g001">Figure 1</a> (other LGs are in Figure S2). <a href="#pone-0001326-t002">Table 2</a> summarizes the state of metacontig anchoring to the genetic map. The 2,093 metacontigs covered 477.1 Mb of genomic DNA. Of these, 435.1 Mb were anchored to the 19 LGs and 81.1% of these were oriented by two or more genetic markers (see Text S1). The smallest LG is covered by 26 metacontigs, the largest by 21 metacontigs. The order of markers established by meiotic recombination-based methods was almost co-linear with the metacontigs. In total, 82% of the genomic sequence was mapped to LGs. Most of the unmapped sequences were contained in 1,696 short metacontigs and singleton contigs with multiple tandem repetitive sequences. The assembly of metacontigs and facilitation of their placement on the genome using a genetic map avoided issues related to physical mapping.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0001326-g001" name="pone-0001326-g001"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g001"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g001" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g001"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g001" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">

          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t002">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t002">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t002">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t002">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t002">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t002">

              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t002</a></p></div></div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2c" name="s2c" class="link-target" title="Gene annotation and gene content"></a>
<h3>Gene annotation and gene content</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Five quality levels were adopted for transcript assignment (see <a href="#s3">Materials and Methods</a>): i) transcripts confirmed by tentative consensus sequences (TCs) and gene predictions (8,110); ii) transcripts confirmed by TCs aligned to the genome (8,160) and among transcripts not confirmed by TC; iii) the retained transcripts predicted at the exon level by different methods (4,028); iv) transcripts which were positive in gene prediction methods with differences at the exon level but with correct gene boundaries (308); v) transcripts which were found by different methods with contrasting results: only genes encoding proteins with significant similarities to known proteins were accepted (8,979). In total 29,585 genes were predicted. Grape gene content is comparable to Arabidopsis (26,819) and markedly different compared with rice (41,046) and poplar (45,555) genomes.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Gene annotation followed a consensus approach. More than 79% of the genes predicted for the grape genome were annotated. Conserved putative grape genes were searched by the BLAST program with rice, poplar and Arabidopsis as references. A decision tree was implemented and used to carry this out. Sets of gene clusters with different levels of similarities among species as well as unique and putative species-specific genes were built. Using strict rules for homology determination, the subset of grape specific genes amounted to 16,859 (<a href="#pone-0001326-g002">Figure 2</a>).</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0001326-g002" name="pone-0001326-g002"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g002"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g002" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g002"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g002" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g002">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g002">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g002">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g002">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g002">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g002">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Figure 2. </span> Comparison of four plant genomes based on gene homology.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.fig1.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.fig1.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>All genes were compared each other as all-<em>vs-</em>all similarity searches using BLAST. Genes predicted for poplar, Arabidopsis and rice are respectively from <a href="http://www.genome.jgi-psf.org">www.genome.jgi-psf.org</a>; <a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org">www.arabidopsis.org</a>; <a href="http://www.tigr.org">www.tigr.org</a>. Grape gene estimates have been carried out on 58,611 assembled contigs. Genes of similar length with over 60% of similarity alignments at protein level were considered homologous using BLOSUM62 matrix <a href="#pone.0001326-Henikoff1" class="ref-tip">[123]</a>. The frequencies of sequences shared among species are reported on the right.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g002">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g002</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Functional classification of the predicted genes was carried out by an automatic procedure. The manually revised final classification (Figure S3) shows the functional classes and their percentage in the gene set. Putative grape-specific genes were not characterized by a particular annotation profile or by relative abundance in the functional classes. A slight numerical difference in favour of grape was noted for genes related to lignin biosynthesis and to berry specific pectins. These metabolic pathways are less significant in Arabidopsis and poplar respectively. Genes relative to disease resistance and wine quality are discussed in further detail below.</p>
</div>

<div id="section4" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2d" name="s2d" class="link-target" title="Disease resistance genes"></a>
<h3>Disease resistance genes</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Resistance to parasites in plants is controlled by the non-host and gene-for-gene pathways <a href="#pone.0001326-Jones1" class="ref-tip">[16]</a>. The non-host type was discovered only recently <a href="#pone.0001326-Lipka1" class="ref-tip">[17]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Stein1" class="ref-tip">[18]</a>. The gene-to-gene pathway is frequently present in cultivated plants displaying dominant resistance genes, responsible for the initiation of signal transduction leading to deployment of defense mechanisms <a href="#pone.0001326-Dangl1" class="ref-tip">[19]</a>. The majority of R proteins contain a nucleotide binding site (NBS) and a carboxy-terminal leucine-rich repeat (LRR) domain. The NBS is part of a conserved domain acting as a molecular switch for the signal transduction. The LRR is credited with recognition specificity akin to an antibody-like detector of pathogen effectors <a href="#pone.0001326-Takken1" class="ref-tip">[20]</a>. At the N-terminus NBS-LRR proteins carry either the coiled coil (CC) domain or a domain homologous to the Toll/Interleukin-1 Receptor (TIR, <a href="#pone.0001326-Meyers1" class="ref-tip">[21]</a>), allowing classification of NBS genes into two groups, the CC-NBS-LRR, present in all angiosperms, and the TIR-NBS-LRR, specific to dicotyledonous species <a href="#pone.0001326-Bai1" class="ref-tip">[22]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Based on resistance domain analyses, the grape genome was found to contain 341 NBS genes (<a href="#pone-0001326-g003">Figure 3</a> and Table S3), whereas 207 were found in Arabidopsis <a href="#pone.0001326-Meyers1" class="ref-tip">[21]</a> and 398 in poplar <a href="#pone.0001326-Tuskan1" class="ref-tip">[12]</a>. The 233 NBS genes which contain the LRR domain can be grouped in 5 major clades (1 to 5 in <a href="#pone-0001326-g003">Figure 3A</a>). The clades were comprised of CC-NBS-LRR, the dictot-specific TIR-NBS-LRR and their truncated structures as follows: (1) mainly TIR-NBS-LRR; (2) and (3) mainly CC-NBS-LRR; (4) mainly NBS-LRR; and (5) CC-NBS-LRR. The CC-NBS-LRR group included 84 genes in grape, 51 in Arabidopsis and 119 in poplar, while the TIR-NBS-LRR group included 37 genes in grape, 64 in poplar and 83 in Arabidopsis. In addition, the grape NBS gene family included 5 truncated TIR-NBS genes, 112 truncated NBS-LRR genes and 103 genes characterized only by the NBS domain (Table S3).</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0001326-g003" name="pone-0001326-g003"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g003"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g003" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g003"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g003" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g003">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g003">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g003">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g003">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g003">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g003">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Figure 3. </span> Chromosomal organization of disease resistance genes of <em>V. vinifera</em>.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.fig1.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.fig1.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>A) Phylogenetic analysis of NBS-LRR protein sequences of <em>V. vinifera</em> present in Pinot Noir. The phylogeny of these genes is based on a distance-matrix neighbour-joining analysis (Clustal X, <a href="#pone.0001326-Thompson1" class="ref-tip">[124]</a>; bootstrap of 1000) after alignment of sequences by TCoffee (version 5.05, <a href="#pone.0001326-NotredameC1" class="ref-tip">[125]</a>). The phylogenetic clades, in general, correspond to the classification based on protein domains (however, see text and Table S3). B) Genes assigned to LGs are represented by dots. Their gene number is specified in LG-specific insets and in Table S3. NBS clades (see A above) contain mainly genes of the following classes: (1) TIR-NBS-LRR in blue; (2) CC-NBS-LRRa in green; (3) CC-NBS-LRRb in yellow; (4) NBS-LRR in cyan; (5) CC-NBS-LRR in red. Other resistance genes, belonging to NBS and TIR-NBS groups, are represented by the open and filled dots, respectively. Resistance-related genes different from NBS genes are shown in black. The size of each LG is given in Mb (on the right), whereas markers of the genetic map (<a href="#pone.0001326-Troggio1" class="ref-tip">[15]</a> and <a href="http://genomics.research.iasma.it">http://genomics.research.iasma.it</a>) are shown on the left, together with the interval in cM between the two closest markers in each gene cluster.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g003">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g003</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Besides NBS genes, the grape genome contains several signalling components of plant disease response which are encoded by genes <em>EDS1</em>, <em>PAD4</em>, <em>COI1</em>, <em>MPK4, JAR1, ETR1</em> and <em>NDR1,</em> known to be recruited by resistance gene products (Table S3). The <em>NPR1</em> gene, a regulator of the systemic acquired response to pathogens <a href="#pone.0001326-Grant1" class="ref-tip">[23]</a>, is present in one copy in grape and in Arabidopsis, but has five copies in poplar. Likewise, <em>RAR1</em> and <em>EIN2</em> are present in single copies in the grape genome.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>Genes encoding the pathogenesis-related proteins (PRs, <a href="#pone.0001326-vanLoon1" class="ref-tip">[24]</a>) include nine copies of <em>PR-1</em>, eight of <em>PR-2</em>, five of <em>PR-3</em>, one copy of <em>PDF1</em>, one of <em>PDF2</em>, and several copies of <em>PR5</em> and protease inhibitor-like genes (<a href="#pone-0001326-g003">Figure 3B</a> and Table S3).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p5" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>In addition, the grape genome contains eight genes similar to the <em>MLO</em> gene for mildew resistance in barley, compared to the 15 <em>MLO-like</em> genes known for Arabidopsis <a href="#pone.0001326-Chen1" class="ref-tip">[25]</a>. MLO proteins belong to a large family of seven-transmembrane domain proteins specific to plants, encoded by genes homologous to barley <em>MLO</em> <a href="#pone.0001326-Chen1" class="ref-tip">[25]</a>. <em>MLO</em> recessive alleles confer an effective resistance against mildew pathogens. Furthermore, the powdery mildew non-host resistance-related genes <em>PEN1</em>, <em>PEN2</em> and <em>PEN3</em> <a href="#pone.0001326-Lipka1" class="ref-tip">[17]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Stein1" class="ref-tip">[18]</a> were found in 5, 5 and 10 copies, respectively.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p6" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p6" class="link-target"></a><p>In grape, the disease-related genes represent a significant part of the genome. In spite of this, many grape varieties, including Pinot Noir, are susceptible to several fungi, such as grey mould (<em>Botrytis cinerea</em>), downy mildew <a href="#pone.0001326-Richter1" class="ref-tip">[26]</a> and powdery mildew <a href="#pone.0001326-Akkurt1" class="ref-tip">[27]</a>, which have to be kept under control by heavy fungicide treatments. The failure to mount an effective defense response is probably due to a defective pathogen recognition. It is known that NBS-LRR genes are undergoing diversifying selection <a href="#pone.0001326-Meyers2" class="ref-tip">[28]</a>, e.g., variation in the sequence of the Arabidopsis gene <em>RPS2</em> shows a signature consistent with pathogen-stimulated selection <a href="#pone.0001326-Mauricio1" class="ref-tip">[29]</a>. Moreover, the extent of variation in the activity of NBS-LRR genes may have been affected by balancing selection <a href="#pone.0001326-Stahl1" class="ref-tip">[30]</a>–<a href="#pone.0001326-Clark1" class="ref-tip">[33]</a>. Grape alleles of the same resistance genes did not co-evolve in the presence of the agriculturally most important grape pathogens <a href="#pone.0001326-DiGaspero1" class="ref-tip">[34]</a>. Indeed, allelic variation due to SNPs present in functional resistance domains was associated with the phenotypic divergence between resistant and susceptible genotypes only when susceptible <em>V. vinifera</em> and resistant non<em>-vinifera</em> clones were considered <a href="#pone.0001326-DiGaspero1" class="ref-tip">[34]</a>. In addition, the long time interval necessary for the grape to complete one generation, together with its vegetative propagation, makes it difficult to match the evolutionary rates of microbial or insect pests, which in vineyards are boosted by massive use of chemicals <a href="#pone.0001326-Belhadj1" class="ref-tip">[35]</a>. Such detailed knowledge of the grape genome will serve to accelerate the development of genetic strategies to counter crop loss due to dynamic and genetically diverse pathogens.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p7" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p7" class="link-target"></a><p>The TIR-NBS-LRR genes are preferentially located in LG 18, the CC-NBS-LRR genes in LGs 9 and 13 and the truncated NBS genes in LGs 12 and 13 (<a href="#pone-0001326-g003">Figure 3B</a>). The NBS genes are also organized in clusters and superclusters. As noted in Arabidopsis <a href="#pone.0001326-Leister1" class="ref-tip">[36]</a>, each cluster may include NBS genes of different phylogenetic lineages, although they frequently consist of tandem repeats of the same gene. The heterogeneity of NBS clusters has been discussed and interpreted as a consequence of evolutionary events such as ectopic recombination, chromosomal translocation and gene-cluster remobilization. This type of genome evolution is difficult to explain other than in terms of a hypothesis where a positive selection for cluster complexity provides the basic materials for the generation of new resistance specificities <a href="#pone.0001326-Richly1" class="ref-tip">[37]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p8" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p8" class="link-target"></a><p>Several clusters of NBS genes mapped to chromosomal regions where genetic resistance to fungal diseases, such as downy and powdery mildew, were previously assigned (<a href="#pone-0001326-g003">Figure 3B</a>). This included LGs 12 and 18 <a href="#pone.0001326-Fischer1" class="ref-tip">[38]</a> and LGs 14 and 15 <a href="#pone.0001326-Akkurt1" class="ref-tip">[27]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Fischer1" class="ref-tip">[38]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Dalb1" class="ref-tip">[39]</a>. Thus, the genome sequence of grape indicates candidate NBS genes responsible for extant variation and provides a starting point for breeding grape varieties resistant to important pathogens.</p>
</div>

<div id="section5" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2e" name="s2e" class="link-target" title="Phenolic and terpenoid pathways"></a>
<h3>Phenolic and terpenoid pathways</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Grape secondary metabolites, particularly polyphenols, have a strong influence on wine quality <a href="#pone.0001326-Waterhouse1" class="ref-tip">[40]</a>. Most phenolics derive from phenylalanine via phenylalanine ammonia-lyase (PAL). They encompass a range of structural classes and biological functions and include lignins, phenolic acids such as hydroxycinnamic and hydroxybenzoic acids, and polyphenols such as flavonoids and stilbenes.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Flavonoids are the most common plant phenolics. In flowers and fruits they attract pollinators and seed dispersers and are particularly involved in UV-scavenging and disease resistance <a href="#pone.0001326-WinkelShirley1" class="ref-tip">[41]</a>. Flavonoids contribute to human health <a href="#pone.0001326-Scalbert1" class="ref-tip">[42]</a>. The flavonoid skeleton, synthesized by chalcone synthase (CHS), is converted to chalcones, flavanones, flavonols, flavanols, anthocyanins and proanthocyanidins (condensed tannins). In red grape, flavanols and anthocyanins are abundant, the latter accumulating mostly in the berry skin and the former in the seeds <a href="#pone.0001326-Mattivi1" class="ref-tip">[43]</a>. In the last decade considerable effort has been made in identifying and cloning grape flavonoid biosynthetic genes <a href="#pone.0001326-Sparvoli1" class="ref-tip">[44]</a>–<a href="#pone.0001326-Fujita1" class="ref-tip">[47]</a>. The grape genome sequence now offers the opportunity of compiling an exhaustive overview of the phenylpropanoid pathway.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Gene predictions corresponding to all those genes known to encode enzymes of the pathway could now be found. These include C4H and 4CL (acronyms are explicated in note 1 of <a href="#pone-0001326-g004">Figure 4A</a>) which were not previously identified in grape. The majority of genes were organized in large (PAL, F3'5'H) or small (CHS, F3H, FLS, LAR) gene families, the remainder consisting of single copy genes (C4H, 4CL, CHI, F3'H, DFR, LDOX, ANR, UFGT) (<a href="#pone-0001326-g004">Figure 4A</a>; Table S4).</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0001326-g004" name="pone-0001326-g004"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g004"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g004" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g004"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g004" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g004">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g004">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g004">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g004">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g004">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g004">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Figure 4. </span> <em>V. vinifera</em> pathways for phenolic and terpenoid biosynthesis.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.fig1.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.fig1.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>A) <em>V. vinifera</em> general pathway for phenolics biosynthesis leading to stilbenes (C6-C2-C6) and flavonoids (C6-C3-C6). For each enzyme, the gene copy number is reported in brackets. Genes were identified by similarity search using BLAST where the references were the sequences of phenolic biosynthetic genes previously characterized in grape and in other plant species. Putative homologues and gene copy numbers were determined by comparing aligned amino acid sequences based on a threshold of 80% similarity between the grape sequences, and 60% similarity between grape and other species. For the large StSy, PAL and F3'5'H families, phylogenetic analysis was performed with MEGA4 package <a href="#pone.0001326-Tamura1" class="ref-tip">[126]</a> after aligning with ClustalW <a href="#pone.0001326-Thompson2" class="ref-tip">[127]</a>. The following enzymes involved in the pathway are shown: PAL, phenylalanine ammonia-lyase; C4H, cinnamate 4-hydroxylase; 4CL, 4-coumarate-CoA ligase; CHS, chalcone synthase; StSy, stilbene synthase; RSGT, resveratrol glucosyltransferase; CHI, chalcone isomerase; F3H, flavanone 3-hydroxylase; F3'H, flavonoid 3′-hydroxylase; F3'5'H, flavonoid 3′,5′-hydroxylase; DFR, dihydroflavonol-4-reductase; FLS, flavonol synthase; LDOX, leucoanthocyanidin dioxygenase; LAR, leucoanthocyanidin reductase; ANR, anthocyanidin reductase; UFGT, UDP-glucose:flavonoid 3-<em>O</em>-glucosyltransferase; OMT, <em>O</em>-methyltransferase; ACCase, acetyl CoA carboxylase. PA refers to proanthocyanidins. Enzymatic steps that have not been experimentally confirmed are marked with an asterisk (*). B) Steps of plastidic isoprenoid pathway and monoterpenoids biosynthesis. For each enzyme, the gene copy number is reported in brackets. Gene annotation was performed as described in <a href="#s3">Material and methods</a>. Abbreviations: G3P, glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate; DXP, 1-deoxy-D-xylulose-5-phosphate; MEP, 2-C-methyl-D-erythritol 4-phosphate; CDP-ME, 4-diphosphocytidyl-2C-methyl-D-erythritol; CDP-MEP, 4-diphosphocytidyl-2Cmethyl-D-erythritol 2-phosphate; ME-cPP, 2C-ethyl-erythritol 2,4-cyclodiphosphate; HMBPP, 1-hydroxy-2-methyl-2-(E)-butenyl 4-diphosphate; IPP, isopentenyl pyrophosphate; DMAPP, dimethylallyl pyrophosphate. The enzymes in the pathway are indicated in blue: DXS, 1-deoxy-D-xylulose 5-phosphate synthase; DXR 1-deoxy-D-xylulose 5-phosphate reductase; ISPD, 4-diphosphocytidyl-2-C-methyl-D-erythritol synthase; ISPE, 4-diphosphocytidyl-2-C-methyl-D-erythritol kinase; ISPF, 2-C-methyl-D-erythritol 2,4-cyclodiphosphate synthase; ISPG, 2-C-methyl-D-erythritol 2,4-cyclodiphosphate synthase and ISPH 2-C-methyl-D-erythritol 2,4-cyclodiphosphate reductase (ISPG and ISPH are probably the same enzyme and convert directly MEcPP in IPP and DMAPP); ISPA, geranyltransferase; IDI, isopentenyl diphosphate delta-isomerase; PT, prenyltransferase; LIMS, limonene synthase; LIS, linalool synthase; GES, geraniol synthase; TES, α-terpineol synthase; TPS-CIN, myrcene/(E)-beta-ocimene synthase; CYTP450, cytochrome P450 hydroxylase; ALDH, aldehyde dehydrogenase; NADPDH, NADP dehydrogenase. Enzymatic steps that have not been experimentally confirmed are marked with an asterisk (*).</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g004">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g004</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>Within the phenylpropanoid pathway, relatively large gene families have been described for poplar compared to Arabidopsis <a href="#pone.0001326-Tsai1" class="ref-tip">[48]</a>. Our results highlight some significant differences, such as the number of PAL and F3'5'H gene copies which were even greater in grape. In general, grape and poplar secondary metabolism exhibits a tendency toward gene family expansion. Conversely, in Arabidopsis all enzymes of the central flavonoid metabolism, except for FLS, are encoded by single genes <a href="#pone.0001326-WinkelShirley1" class="ref-tip">[41]</a>. This is consistent with the noted low metabolic investment in flavonoids of Arabidopsis, a species which reproduces without the need for insect pollination and has no perennial woody habit.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p5" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>In grape, as in a few other species, the condensation of p-coumaroyl-CoA with malonyl-CoA gives rise to stilbenes via stilbene synthase (StSy; <a href="#pone.0001326-Schroder1" class="ref-tip">[49]</a>). Among stilbenes, monomers and oligomers (viniferins) of resveratrol contribute to resistance to fungal pathogens <a href="#pone.0001326-Jeandet1" class="ref-tip">[50]</a>. Resveratrol has gained attention due to its alleged beneficial effects on human health <a href="#pone.0001326-Baur1" class="ref-tip">[51]</a>. Stilbene synthase belongs to a large family: the analysis of the grape genome predicts at least 21 copies. This number agrees well with a recent StSy sequence analysis in infected grape leaves <a href="#pone.0001326-Richter1" class="ref-tip">[26]</a> but it differs from the one predicted in the PN40024 grape genome sequence <a href="#pone.0001326-Jaillon1" class="ref-tip">[7]</a>. Most of these copies, as well as most PAL genes, are clustered in LG 16. Further, several peroxidase genes were predicted, some of which could participate in the formation of viniferins, as previously suggested <a href="#pone.0001326-Jeandet1" class="ref-tip">[50]</a>. Recently, a resveratrol glucosyltransferase putatively involved in piceid synthesis has been isolated and biochemically characterized in <em>V. labrusca</em> grape berry <a href="#pone.0001326-Hall1" class="ref-tip">[52]</a>. Our analysis revealed that its homolog in Pinot Noir (99% sequence similarity) is present as a single gene mapping on LG 3.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p6" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p6" class="link-target"></a><p>Terpenoids are among the most abundant and structurally diverse group of natural metabolites. Volatile and non-volatile terpenes are essential for plant growth and development (e.g., gibberellin phytohormones), but they are also key players in the interaction of plants with the environment <a href="#pone.0001326-Tholl1" class="ref-tip">[53]</a>. The substrates for the biosynthesis of about 22,000 terpenes are isopentenyl diphosphate (IPP) and dimethylallyl diphosphate (DMAPP). The mevalonate (MVA) and the mevalonate-independent DOXP/MEP pathways are responsible for the synthesis of IPP and DMAPP in the cytosolic and plastidic compartments respectively <a href="#pone.0001326-Lichtenthaler1" class="ref-tip">[54]</a>. DOXP/MEP is the dominant route for monoterpene biosynthesis in the grape berry <a href="#pone.0001326-Luan1" class="ref-tip">[55]</a>. Three prenyltransferases produce terpene precursors, prenyl diphosphates, geranyl diphosphate (GPP), farnesyl diphosphate (FPP) and geranylgeranyl diphosphate (GGPP). Terpene synthases (TPS) catalyze the formation of hemiterpenes <a href="#pone.0001326-Baur1" class="ref-tip">[51]</a>, monoterpenes (C10), sesquiterpenes (C15) or diterpenes (C20) from the substrates DMAPP, GPP, FPP or GGPP respectively (<a href="#pone-0001326-g004">Figure 4B</a>).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p7" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p7" class="link-target"></a><p>All TPSs are similar in physico-chemical properties. Moreover, the close sequence relatedness of their genes prevents discrimination of their catalytic functions, supporting a rapid divergence of catalytic activity of closely related TPS genes <a href="#pone.0001326-Tholl1" class="ref-tip">[53]</a>. Three classes of TPSs are described and only classes II and III are specific for the plant secondary metabolism <a href="#pone.0001326-Bohlmann1" class="ref-tip">[56]</a>. Forty seven TPS genes participate in the secondary metabolism in poplar <a href="#pone.0001326-Tuskan1" class="ref-tip">[12]</a>, while in grape only 35 TPSs were identified, a number close to the 32 found in Arabidopsis. In the grape genome, they are located mainly on LGs 9, 10 and 19 (class I TPs on LGs 7, 9, 10 and 19, Table S5).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p8" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p8" class="link-target"></a><p>Several higher plant genes of the terpenoid pathways have been cloned <a href="#pone.0001326-McCaskill1" class="ref-tip">[57]</a>, but only a few of them had previously been identified in grape <a href="#pone.0001326-Martin1" class="ref-tip">[58]</a>. Having the complete sequence of the grape genome, 124 genes related to the terpenoid pathway were identified (Table S5). Of these, 110 were mapped to all LGs. Functionally, 24 are related to carotenoids, 24 to abscisic acid metabolism, 10 to gibberellin hormones, and 6 cover steps of the core terpenoids pathway: 5 prenyltransferases and 1 isopentenyl diphosphate delta-isomerase. For the MVA and non-MVA pathways, nine (4 DXS, DXR, ISPD, ISPE, ISPF, ISPH) and eight (2 AACT, HMGS, 3 HMGR, MK/MVK, MVD) putative genes were identified respectively.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p9" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p9" class="link-target"></a><p>Plant monoterpens are preferentially confined to specialized organs. They play an important role in defense as well as acting as allelopathic agents and attractants for pollinators <a href="#pone.0001326-Harborne1" class="ref-tip">[59]</a>. In grape, monoterpenes contribute to wine free volatiles: typical components of the aroma-rich grape varieties are linalool, geraniol, nerol, citronellol and α-terpineol, which are stored in exocarps and vacuoles. Monoterpene biosynthesis has not yet been studied because several metabolic steps may take place without enzymatic catalysis. Moreover, the knowledge of mechanisms controlling monoterpene synthase activity is still largely incomplete. In the grape genome four monoterpene synthase genes were identified encoding linalool synthase, limonene synthase, myrcene synthase and α-terpineol synthase.</p>
</div>

<div id="section6" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2f" name="s2f" class="link-target" title="Transcription factors"></a>
<h3>Transcription factors</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>In grape, 2004 TF genes were identified (<a href="#pone-0001326-g005">Figure 5A</a> and Table S6) which represent 6.7 % of the genome, similar to the 6% for Arabidopsis <a href="#pone.0001326-Riechmann1" class="ref-tip">[60]</a>, 4.8 % for rice <a href="#pone.0001326-Yu1" class="ref-tip">[61]</a> and 6% for poplar <a href="#pone.0001326-Zhu1" class="ref-tip">[62]</a>. Among the grape TF genes, 80.6% are present in marker-anchored metacontigs (<a href="#pone-0001326-g005">Figure 5A</a>).</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0001326-g005" name="pone-0001326-g005"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g005"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g005" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g005"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g005" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">

          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g005">

                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g005">

              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g005">

                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g005">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g005">

TF types are reported on the right.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g005">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g005</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Sixty-two families of TF genes were found, a number similar to the 64 for Arabidopsis, 62 for rice and 63 for poplar <a href="#pone.0001326-RianoPachon1" class="ref-tip">[63]</a>. TF families like MYB, AP2/EREBP, bHLH and MADS-box include a large number of members <a href="#pone.0001326-Goff1" class="ref-tip">[11]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Riechmann1" class="ref-tip">[60]</a>. We compared the number of genes in each of the 60 grape TF families in common to the other three plant genomes: finding a nearly linear correlation (<a href="#pone-0001326-g006">Figure 6</a>). Thus the organization and number of TFs seem to be highly conserved in plant genomes. TF distribution in the grape genome (<a href="#pone-0001326-g005">Figure 5A</a>) indicates that only LGs 7 and 18 have a higher than average TF content. Clusters of AP2/EREBP genes are repeated in tandem on LGs 2, 7, 10 and 16; CCAAT genes on LGs 6, 8, 10 and 13; MADS-box genes on LGs 5 and 13; Myb genes on LG 8 and 17 (<a href="#pone-0001326-g005">Figure 5B</a>).</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0001326-g006" name="pone-0001326-g006"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g006"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g006" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g006"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g006" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g006">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g006">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g006">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g006">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g006">
