                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g006">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Figure 6. </span> Scatter plot of the distribution of <em>V. vinifera</em> transcription factors.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.fig2.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.fig2.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>For each of the 60 families (1983 genes) of <em>V. vinifera</em> TFs (X-axis) (log base 2 transformed), family members have been plotted against the corresponding number reported for three other genomes: A) <em>A. thaliana</em> (<a href="http://arabtfdb.bio.uni-potsdam.de/v1.1">http://arabtfdb.bio.uni-potsdam.de/v1.1</a>), B) <em>P. trichocarpa</em> (<a href="http://poplartfdb.bio.uni-potsdam.de/v2.0">http://poplartfdb.bio.uni-potsdam.de/v2.0</a>) and C) <em>O. sativa</em> (<a href="http://ricetfdb.bio.uni-potsdam.de/v2.i">http://ricetfdb.bio.uni-potsdam.de/v2.i</a>). The degree of the correlation among TF gene numbers is indicated by the Pearson correlation value (r). Each scatter plot shows the TF families which were statistically over- or under- represented in pair-wise comparisons (χ<sup>2</sup> tests were applied to untransformed data; p = 0.05).</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g006">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g006</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Across the species mentioned, MYB (279) are the most abundant <a href="#pone.0001326-Goff1" class="ref-tip">[11]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Shiu1" class="ref-tip">[64]</a>. They play a role in controlling the accumulation of secondary metabolites in the grape berry <a href="#pone.0001326-Deluc1" class="ref-tip">[65]</a>–<a href="#pone.0001326-Bogs3" class="ref-tip">[67]</a>. A gene from this TF family is also known to play a key role in the regulation of anthocyanins and flavonols during the non-climacteric ripening of strawberry <a href="#pone.0001326-Aharoni1" class="ref-tip">[68]</a>. Non-climacteric ripening (occurring in fruit such as strawberry and grapevine) is a process characterized by the absence in respiratory pick and ethylene bursts, two phenomena typical of the climacteric fruits ripening.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>In the grape genome were also found 143 leucine-zipper genes. Together with EREBP TFs they contribute to the plant's defense response <a href="#pone.0001326-Buttner1" class="ref-tip">[69]</a>. In tepary and common bean, a bZIP gene plays an important role in the response to water deficit and in the regulation of abscisic acid levels. <a href="#pone.0001326-RodriguezUribe1" class="ref-tip">[70]</a></p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.p5" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>In the grape genome, the MADS-box family is also over-represented. These TFs regulate flowering-related phenomena, as well as other metabolisms <a href="#pone.0001326-AlvarezBuylla1" class="ref-tip">[71]</a>. MADS-box TFs may have been important during plant evolution because they allow plant reproductive structures to adapt to variations in climatic conditions <a href="#pone.0001326-Ng1" class="ref-tip">[72]</a>. It was found that two tandem MADS-box genes (MADS-RIN and MADS-MC) regulate fruit ripening and inflorescence determinancy in the climacteric fruit tomato. Mutation at <em>rin</em> locus caused a failure in the normal ripening physiology <a href="#pone.0001326-Vrebalov1" class="ref-tip">[73]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.p6" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec6.p6" class="link-target"></a><p>A ripening mechanism common to both climacteric and non-climacteric species, such as grape, has been hypothesized <a href="#pone.0001326-Giovannoni1" class="ref-tip">[74]</a>. In support of this, we identified two TF classes in grape, AP2/EREBP and EIL, which contribute to ethylene signalling during ripening of climacteric fruits, and also found ethylene receptors belonging to ETR/ERS families.</p>
</div>

<div id="section7" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2g" name="s2g" class="link-target" title="Repetitive elements"></a>
<h3>Repetitive elements</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec7.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec7.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Matching the sequences of assembled contigs with original reads made it possible to characterize each DNA segment by the number of matching reads (see <a href="#s3">Materials and methods</a>). For the read coverage of 10.7X, a DNA segment was considered unique when represented by 15 or fewer matches. Moderately repeated sequences (2 to 8 copies per genome) were expected to have 16–100 matches. Sequences with more than 100 matches were considered highly repetitive. They were masked before gene prediction, thus excluding most of the coding parts of repetitive elements from the putative gene set.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec7.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec7.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Dispersed highly repetitive DNA sequences were identified by an iterative procedure, and the resulting collection of 90,483 repetitive segments were grouped into 136 types. Members of each type were translated and compared to each other and the similarity scores were used in a UPGMA-like clustering. The similarity tree consisted of eight clusters lacking a common root (Figure S4), each of which was assigned to the known classes of repetitive DNA sequences (Table S7).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec7.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec7.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Grape transposable elements (TEs), totalling 108.5 Mb, represent the most abundant set of repeats. The repeats were included in group I (retrotransposons: Copia, Gypsy, LINE) and group II (DNA transposons: Mutator, CACTA, hAT) according to Feschotte et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Feschotte1" class="ref-tip">[75]</a>. The most abundant TEs were Gypsy/athila-like elements followed by Copia elements. DNA transposons were represented by 9,562 copies (7.1 Mb). The TEs seem to be more abundant in grape compared to poplar <a href="#pone.0001326-Tuskan1" class="ref-tip">[12]</a>, Arabidopsis and rice <a href="#pone.0001326-Goff1" class="ref-tip">[11]</a>. Putatively autonomous TEs were identified by significant BLAST analysis against the Uniprot database. TEs without a significant BLAST hit were attributed to the non-autonomous group (Table S7). Out of 136 repeat types, 20 were classified as long tandem repeats with a unit size from 100 to 430 bp. They were grouped into ten major sub-classes.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec7.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec7.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>Short tandem repeats (microsatellites) were also identified. Their thresholds, number of copies and total DNA length are reported in Table S8. Microsatellites cover 2.1 Mb, including the telomeric repeats (TTTAGGG). Out of 171 contigs with identified telomeric sequences, 42 had telomeric ends. In the linkage map, they represent potential markers for telomeres.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec7.p5" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec7.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>An alternative estimation of the length of identified repetitive DNA was performed using the number and total length of reads matching repeat sequences, identified above. This new estimate gave a value of 138.5 Mb, corresponding to 27.4% of the 504.6 Mb genome size.</p>
</div>

<div id="section8" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2h" name="s2h" class="link-target" title="Non-coding RNAs"></a>
<h3>Non-coding RNAs</h3>

<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2h1" name="s2h1" class="link-target" title="MicroRNAs"></a><h4>MicroRNAs.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>MicroRNAs (miRNAs) and trans-acting siRNAs (ta-siRNAs) have a significant role in plant development and stress response <a href="#pone.0001326-JonesRhoades1" class="ref-tip">[76]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Sunkar1" class="ref-tip">[77]</a>. The majority of the 1220 plant miRNAs listed in the miRBAse <a href="#pone.0001326-GriffithsJones1" class="ref-tip">[78]</a> are from Arabidopsis (184), rice (243) and poplar (215). A BLAST search of sequences similar to the Arabidopsis miRNAs genes was performed on the grape genome. Allowing for three or fewer mismatches, 143 miRNA genes representing 28 families (<a href="#pone.0001326-GriffithsJones1" class="ref-tip">[78]</a>; <a href="#pone-0001326-t003">Table 3</a>, Table S9) were identified.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0001326-t003" name="pone-0001326-t003"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t003"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t003" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t003"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t003" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t003">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t003">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t003">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t003">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t003">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t003">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Table 3. </span> Distribution of miRNA encoding genes of <em>V. vinifera</em> on LGs and number of their putative target genes grouped in families.</div><p class="caption_target"></p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t003">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.t003</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec1.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec1.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Three types of miRNAs (miR827, miR828 and miR846) were not previously found outside Arabidopsis, and were considered “non-conserved” miRNAs <a href="#pone.0001326-Rajagopalan1" class="ref-tip">[79]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Fahlgren1" class="ref-tip">[80]</a>. However, these genes are present in the grape genome, indicating that they were either lost in the lineage leading to <em>Populus</em> or are missing from its genome assembly. The miRNA passenger strands (miRNAs*) are highly conserved between grape and Arabidopsis <a href="#pone.0001326-Rajagopalan1" class="ref-tip">[79]</a>. Sequences predicted to produce ta-siRNAs <a href="#pone.0001326-Axtell1" class="ref-tip">[81]</a> are conserved in several plant species, grape included.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec1.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec1.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Putative grape miRNAs and siRNAs target the same classes of genes as they do in Arabidopsis, rice and poplar: transcription factor genes, genes involved in stress response and nutrient uptake, genes for RNA silencing and the non coding RNA <em>TAS3</em> (<a href="#pone-0001326-t003">Table 3</a>). In grape, 56 RNA-dependent DNA polymerase genes are potentially targeted by miR396 and miR846, a phenomenon not reported in other plant species.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec1.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec1.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>BLAST searches identified four Dicer-like proteins (Helicase, RNAse IIIa/b domains), nine <em>Argonautes</em> (PAZ/PIWI domains), and six RNA-dependent RNA polymerases (RdRp domains), indicating the presence in the grape genome of a complex RNA processing machinery (Figure S5).</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2h2" name="s2h2" class="link-target" title="Transfer RNA"></a><h4>Transfer RNA.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec2.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The tRNAscan-SE program <a href="#pone.0001326-Lowe1" class="ref-tip">[82]</a> identified 719 putative tRNA genes. 163 of them are pseudogenes, 3 are suppressors for the TAA codon while 553 correspond to 52 anticodons for all amino acids (Table S10).</p>
</div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2h3" name="s2h3" class="link-target" title="Small nuclear RNA"></a><h4>Small nuclear RNA.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec3.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Non-coding RNAs include five major and four minor snRNA families, all components of splicing factors. The Arabidopsis snRNA list of Wang and Brendel <a href="#pone.0001326-Wang2" class="ref-tip">[83]</a> was used to search for similar sequences in grape. We found 89 snRNA genes and pseudogenes (75 in Arabidopsis) (Table S11). Several snRNA genes were clustered in the genome.</p>
</div>

<div id="section4" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2h4" name="s2h4" class="link-target" title="Ribosomal RNA"></a><h4>Ribosomal RNA.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec4.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec4.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Large rRNA units consist of two segments, one hosting the genes 18S rRNA, 5.8S and 28S, the second containing three arrays of tandem repeats. In grape the length of the rRNA unit is around 10.8 Kb. The variable segment includes three arrays of tandem repeats: about 40 copies of a 44–45 bp repeat, three copies of a 150 bp repeat and 5.5 copies of a 193 bp repeat. The unit is repeated 1450–1550 times in the genome (16.1 Mb). rRNA units may contain insertions of retrotransposons of three different lengths (2870, 2950, and 5800 bp). Retroelements in rRNA sequences may cause transposition of rRNA sequences.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec4.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec4.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The DNA sequence for the small ribosomal RNA unit (1,250 bp) contains two genes for 5S rRNA, 120 bp each with a single nucleotide difference between them. In the genome the unit was represented by 170–180 copies. Together, large rRNA and 5S rRNA sequences were estimated to amount to 16.3 Mb.</p>
</div>

<div id="section5" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2h5" name="s2h5" class="link-target" title="Small nucleolar RNA"></a><h4>Small nucleolar RNA.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec5.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec8.sec5.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Based on the Arabidopsis snoRNA genes <a href="#pone.0001326-Brown1" class="ref-tip">[84]</a>, 166 sequences representing 79 families were found in grape (Table S12). Most of the grape snoRNA genes (110) are clustered; 62 snoRNA genes were located inside 34 genes encoding six ribosomal proteins and one eIF-4F factor.</p>
</div>
</div>

<div id="section9" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2i" name="s2i" class="link-target" title="Pinot Noir genome structure and evolution"></a>
<h3>Pinot Noir genome structure and evolution</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The existence of structural diversity between homologous chromosomes within plant species has been reported <a href="#pone.0001326-Brunner1" class="ref-tip">[85]</a>. This type of molecular variation seems to be common in allogamous plants <a href="#pone.0001326-Rafalski1" class="ref-tip">[86]</a> and could also be a characteristic of autogamous species <a href="#pone.0001326-Clark1" class="ref-tip">[33]</a>. Grape does not tolerate long term inbreeding <a href="#pone.0001326-Olmo1" class="ref-tip">[5]</a> and high outcrossing rates maintain the genome in a heterozygous state, as evident in the remarkable variation found in collections of grape varieties <a href="#pone.0001326-This1" class="ref-tip">[87]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The genome sequence data from a cultivated grape variety provides unprecedented insight into the structural nature of heterozygosity in an outcrossing species. The variation within this clone of grape consists largely of chromosome-specific gaps and hemizygous DNA. In addition to the regions in which it was possible to merge haplotypes representing DNA from both chromosomes in a consensus sequence, regions were found which were chromosome-specific, i.e., either with different DNA sequence flanked by orthologous regions of the two homologous chromosomes (hemizygous DNA) or gaps corresponding to sequences absent in one chromosome but not in the other. One million gaps, covering 48.9 Mb, and 65.1 Mp corresponding to hemizygous DNA distributed in 22,610 contigs were identified. These data allow us to conclude that the homologous chromosomes of Pinot Noir differ on average by 11.2 % of their DNA sequences and that the grape genome exists in a dynamic state, mediated at least in part by transposable element activity, as reported for helitron TE <a href="#pone.0001326-Morgante1" class="ref-tip">[88]</a>. Indeed, the large grape genomic gaps are frequently bordered by 5 bp direct repeats, reminiscent of a type of DNA excision mediated by a precise process of transposition <a href="#pone.0001326-Chandler1" class="ref-tip">[89]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>The genomic region represented in <a href="#pone-0001326-g007">Figure 7A</a> highlights the differences which exist between homologous haplotypes. Notable differences in this region concern the presence of gaps and the number of copies of TE.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0001326-g007" name="pone-0001326-g007"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g007"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g007" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g007"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g007" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g007">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g007">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g007">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g007">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g007">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g007">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Figure 7. </span> Features of the Pinot Noir heterozygous genome.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.fig1.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.fig1.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>A) Comparison of constrasting haplotypes (<em>a</em> and <em>b</em>) co-mapping at two almost contigous regions in metacontig 32,921 of chromosome 1. Above: the 188 kb region; below: the 215 kb region. I from contig groups 1030-H15, 1079-G03, 2068-K04, 1034-C17 and II 2010-J07, 2044-L11, 1030-N10. In the genetic map the two regions are positioned at 60.1 cM: see preliminary experiment in Text S1. TE elements are labeled as follows: c: Copia; g: Gypsy/gypsy; a: Gypsy/athila; d: hAT/Dart; k: Karma; h: hAT; m: Mutator. B) SNP profiles of the 19 LGs of <em>V. vinifera</em>. Left and right of the figure correspond respectively to top and bottom of LGs of Troggio et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Troggio1" class="ref-tip">[15]</a>. The SNP values reported do not consider gaps in and among metacontigs. C) SNPs in exons and non-coding DNA and percentage of anchored genes tagged with SNPs. In parts B to E of this figure, gene prediction and annotation and the exon-intron boundaries were based on the methods described in Solovyev et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Solovyev1" class="ref-tip">[114]</a>; Korf et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Korf1" class="ref-tip">[101]</a>; Majoros et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Majoros1" class="ref-tip">[115]</a>; Altschul et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Altschul1" class="ref-tip">[116]</a>; Huang and Madan <a href="#pone.0001326-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[117]</a>. D) Relative age of grape duplicated genes estimated from the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous sites (K<sub>S</sub> values). The peak between 0.6 and 1.2 KS supports a relatively large scale duplication event. Paralog genes were identified as in Li et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Li1" class="ref-tip">[120]</a> and K<sub>S</sub> distributions were calculated as in Maere et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Maere1" class="ref-tip">[105]</a>. E) The same as in D for genes present in duplicated chromosome segments.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g007">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g007</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>In the preliminary experiment (see Text S1), it was found that the frequency of SNPs correlated with deletions and insertions. Segments with less than one in/del per Kb had 4.4 SNPs per Kb, whereas segments with one or more in/del per Kb had 16.7 SNPs per Kb. A total of 2 millions SNPs (1,751,176 anchored and the remaining present in other assembled sequences) were discovered and validated and more than a million in/dels were annotated on the sequence with defined location. Our data allow us to extend the evaluation of nucleotide variation to the entire genome rather than to limited resequenced DNA regions <a href="#pone.0001326-Rafalski1" class="ref-tip">[86]</a>. Among recently sequenced animal genomes, a high SNP frequency was found in sea urchin <a href="#pone.0001326-Sodergren1" class="ref-tip">[90]</a> and <em>Cyona intestinalis</em> <a href="#pone.0001326-Dehal1" class="ref-tip">[91]</a>. Across the grape genetic map (<a href="#pone-0001326-g007">Figure 7B</a>), the SNP frequency had an average value of 4.0 per Kb.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p5" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>Coding and non-coding regions demonstrated different degrees of polymorphism with 2.5 and 5.5 SNPs per Kb respectively. One or more SNPs were found in 86.7% of anchored genes and 71.4% of genes had more than four SNPs (<a href="#pone-0001326-g007">Figure 7C</a>). Those gene-based markers are valuable tools, as SNPs present in functional genes may cause natural phenotypic variation <a href="#pone.0001326-Fridman1" class="ref-tip">[92]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Thornsberry1" class="ref-tip">[93]</a> and help in genetic diagnosis. In addition, we noticed some reduction of SNP frequency in gene desert regions, described for the dog genome <a href="#pone.0001326-LindbladToh1" class="ref-tip">[94]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p6" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p6" class="link-target"></a><p>In several regions of the 19 LGs, SNP frequency peaks between 5 and 7.5 per 1 Kb, even if the frequency may reach values much higher than those cited (<a href="#pone-0001326-g007">Figure 7B</a>). Other regions displayed dramatically reduced frequencies. Therefore, as shown for human <a href="#pone.0001326-Venter1" class="ref-tip">[95]</a>, dog <a href="#pone.0001326-LindbladToh1" class="ref-tip">[94]</a> and <em>Anopheles</em> <a href="#pone.0001326-Holt1" class="ref-tip">[96]</a> genomes, the Pinot Noir chromosomes consist of large blocks where two haplotypes are present. The sparseness of putative quasi-homozygous haplotypic blocks indicates that heterozygosity prevails.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p7" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p7" class="link-target"></a><p>Arabidopsis and poplar have likely undergone three rounds of whole genome duplications during evolution <a href="#pone.0001326-Tuskan1" class="ref-tip">[12]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Simillion1" class="ref-tip">[97]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Bowers1" class="ref-tip">[98]</a>, although this has been challenged recently <a href="#pone.0001326-Jaillon1" class="ref-tip">[7]</a>. The first duplication (referred to as 1R, <a href="#pone.0001326-Bowers1" class="ref-tip">[98]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-DeBodt1" class="ref-tip">[99]</a>) may have predated the divergence of monocots and eudicots, while the second one (2R) probably occurred around the radiation of the core-eudicots prior to the divergence of poplar and Arabidopsis <a href="#pone.0001326-Tuskan1" class="ref-tip">[12]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-DeBodt1" class="ref-tip">[99]</a>. The most recent duplications in poplar and Arabidopsis have occurred after their divergence <a href="#pone.0001326-LindbladToh1" class="ref-tip">[94]</a>. The current thinking is that <em>Vitis</em> is an early diverging lineage within the rosids that has diverged prior to the divergence of Arabidopsis and poplar <a href="#pone.0001326-Jansen1" class="ref-tip">[100]</a>. We determined the relative age of grape duplicated genes from the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (K<sub>S</sub>). The age distribution of <em>Vitis</em> duplicates shows a clear peak of K<sub>S</sub> values between 0.6 and 1.2 suggesting a relatively recent large-scale duplication event (<a href="#pone-0001326-g007">Figure 7D</a>). A smaller peak is also visible for K<sub>S</sub> values between 2.0 and 2.5, probably corresponding to more ancient large-scale duplications, as is the case for poplar and Arabidopsis <a href="#pone.0001326-Korf1" class="ref-tip">[101]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p8" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p8" class="link-target"></a><p>Different approaches were taken to estimate the age of the youngest large-scale duplication event. First, it should be noted that the youngest peak lies to the left of the peak formed by K<sub>S</sub> values between orthologs of <em>Vitis</em> and Arabidopsis (Figure S6) although one should be very cautious in comparing different K<sub>S</sub> distributions due to different substitution rates in different organisms. Second, we also detected duplicated segments, covering about half of the genome, using a previously described method <a href="#pone.0001326-Simillion2" class="ref-tip">[102]</a>. K<sub>S</sub> values of genes in these duplicated blocks (<a href="#pone-0001326-g007">Figure 7E</a>) showed that the majority of these are responsible for the 0.6–1.2 K<sub>S</sub> peak (<a href="#pone-0001326-g007">Figure 7D</a>) and thus likely to be remnants of a single large-scale duplication event. We have also used phylogenetic approaches (see <a href="#s3">Methods</a>) to estimate the relative age of genes in duplicated blocks. In total, 485 gene pairs support duplication prior to the split Arabidopsis–<em>Vitis</em>, while 523 gene pairs support duplication after the divergence of Arabidopsis and <em>Vitis</em>, i.e., are <em>Vitis</em> specific, although distributions of K<sub>S</sub> values for these two sets of genes are not discernable (not shown). When duplicated blocks of which at least two-thirds of the anchors support the same tree topology are considered, almost twice as many blocks support duplication within the <em>Vitis</em> lineage than before the divergence of <em>Vitis</em>. As a matter of fact, we suspect the actual number of genes supporting a <em>Vitis</em>-specific duplication to be higher. Indeed, it has been shown in several studies that, following gene duplication, one of the duplicates evolves at an increased rate <a href="#pone.0001326-VandePeer1" class="ref-tip">[103]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Byrne1" class="ref-tip">[104]</a>. This could easily lead to the inference of erroneous tree topologies where one of the <em>Vitis</em> duplicates branches off earlier than it should, in particular if the duplication event occurred shortly after the specialtion of <em>Vitis</em> (see further).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p9" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p9" class="link-target"></a><p>Jaillon et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Jaillon1" class="ref-tip">[7]</a> propose that three ancestral genomes contributed to the <em>Vitis</em> lineage and suggest ancestral hexaploidization for most eudicots, while not finding evidence for a recent duplication in grape. Furthermore, they suggest that, since their split, poplar has undergone an additional whole genome duplication, while Arabidopsis has undergone two additional genome duplications. These results are at odds with our findings. Reanalysis of Arabidopsis and poplar genomes (not shown) uncovers, for both, many homologous segments with a multiplication level between five and eight, which suggests three rounds of duplications for both genomes <a href="#pone.0001326-Simillion1" class="ref-tip">[97]</a>. If the Arabidopsis and poplar genomes were ancient hexaploids, to which two additional genome duplications had been added, fragment multiplication of up to twelve should be expected for Arabidopsis, and up to six in poplar.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p10" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.p10" class="link-target"></a><p>The fact that there is substantial ambiguity in the dating of the duplicates in duplicated segments suggests that the most recent large-scale duplication event reported here for <em>Vitis</em> might have occurred in close proximity to the <em>Vitis</em> speciation event. Therefore, an alternative scenario than the one presented by Jaillon et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Jaillon1" class="ref-tip">[7]</a> that we would like to put forward is shown in <a href="#pone-0001326-g008">Figure 8</a>. We assume three genome duplications to have occurred in both poplar and Arabidopsis, as proposed earlier <a href="#pone.0001326-Tuskan1" class="ref-tip">[12]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Bowers1" class="ref-tip">[98]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Maere1" class="ref-tip">[105]</a>, one of which has been shared by all dicots (and possible also by the monocots, see <a href="#pone.0001326-Bowers1" class="ref-tip">[98]</a>), one that has been shared by Arabidopsis and poplar, but not <em>Vitis</em>, and one that has been specific to Arabidopsis <a href="#pone.0001326-Bowers1" class="ref-tip">[98]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Maere1" class="ref-tip">[105]</a> and poplar <a href="#pone.0001326-Tuskan1" class="ref-tip">[12]</a>, respectively. Since many regions of the <em>Vitis</em> genome appear in triplicate in both Jaillon et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Jaillon1" class="ref-tip">[7]</a> and our own analyses (not shown), the genome duplication shared by all dicots might have been followed by a hybridization event in <em>Vitis</em>, shortly after its divergence from the lineage leading to poplar and Arabidopsis (see <a href="#pone-0001326-g008">Figure 8</a>).</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0001326-g008" name="pone-0001326-g008"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g008"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g008" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g008"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g008" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g008">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g008">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g008">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g008">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g008">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g008">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Figure 8. </span> Scenario of angiosperm genome evolution.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.fig2.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec9.fig2.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Alternative scenario to the one proposed by Jaillon et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Jaillon1" class="ref-tip">[7]</a> to explain angiosperm genome evolution. Our analyses seem to suggest that there has been a large-scale duplication event, likely a hybridization event, in the <em>Vitis</em> lineage, rather than before the split of <em>Vitis</em> and other dicots. See text for details.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g008">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0001326.g008</a></p></div></div>

<div id="section10" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2j" name="s2j" class="link-target" title="Concluding remarks"></a>
<h3>Concluding remarks</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec10.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec10.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The Grapevine Genome Initiative was established with the aim of accelerating the breeding of a difficult perennial species. Grape breeding for disease resistance, if not for immunity, would be a solution to the problem of the emergence of aggressive races of micro-organisms that are currently controlled by massive use of agrochemicals. The problem is not a simple one: how to modify a complex and highly heterozygous genome without altering wine quality. Precise knowledge of all the genes influencing quality and resistance traits is an absolute prerequisite for such modifications.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec10.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec10.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>A high number of genes related to disease–resistance have been identified; many of them have been mapped to LGs and a large part of them are tagged with one or more SNPs. These resistance genes, however, did not co-evolve in the presence of the most important grape pathogens <a href="#pone.0001326-DiGaspero1" class="ref-tip">[34]</a>, a condition which may have not sufficiently protected the species. This is in part the reason why a deep knowledge of the grape genome is the starting point for developing genetic strategies to counter pathogens.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec10.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec10.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Description of the grape genome sequence opens the opportunity for molecular breeding in grape. The fertility of hybrids between wild and domesticated grape species with 19 seemingly co-linear chromosomes <a href="#pone.0001326-Olmo1" class="ref-tip">[5]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Grando1" class="ref-tip">[106]</a>–<a href="#pone.0001326-Lowe2" class="ref-tip">[108]</a> makes it feasible to introduce new resistance genes via traditional breeding. The NBS gene clusters identified here can be associated with QTLs affecting disease resistance or tolerance behaviour of grape varieties (this is the case with LGs 12, 14, 15 and 18; <a href="#pone.0001326-Akkurt1" class="ref-tip">[27]</a>, <a href="#pone.0001326-Dalb1" class="ref-tip">[39]</a>). This large and underexploited reservoir of resistance genes could be easily moved in clusters across genomes by choosing appropriate molecular markers to selectively introgress only the resistance traits. This would prevent the loss of alleles important for grape and wine quality. Thus, the anchored sequence of the grape genome, together with the large arsenal of SNP loci, now offers a tool to open a new era in the molecular breeding of grape.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec10.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec10.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>WGS using longer read dye-terminator sequences can be combined with shorter SBS sequence data using dedicated assembly programs. Using this method we have resolved a complex heterozygous eukaryotic genome. Future whole genome sequencing efforts should be able to combine these two methods to produce assemblies in shorter times while reducing the need for resources. The ability to resolve the haplotypes in Pinot Noir suggests that sequencing DNA mixtures, for example more than one genotype of a given crop, is practical. Such an approach generates both a consensus sequence of the genome and a set of mapped marker loci to be used in breeding programs.</p>
</div>
</div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3" name="s3" data-toc="s3" class="link-target" title="Materials and Methods"></a><h2>Materials and Methods</h2>
<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3a" name="s3a" class="link-target" title="DNA source"></a>
<h3>DNA source</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>In order to prepare shotgun libraries, DNA was extracted from young shoots of Pinot Noir, clone ENTAV115, randomly sheared and size-selected. Two BAC libraries were also constructed (<a href="#pone.0001326-AdamBlondon1" class="ref-tip">[109]</a>; Keygene, Wageningen, NL) and clones assembled in a physical map (<a href="http://genomics.research.iasma.it">http://genomics.research.iasma.it</a>). A population of 94 F<sub>1</sub> plants from the cross between Syrah and Pinot Noir was the source of the DNA used for mapping markers and anchoring metacontigs.</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3b" name="s3b" class="link-target" title="Libraries"></a>
<h3>Libraries</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec2.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Fosmid and shotgun libraries were from DNA purified by a CTAB method <a href="#pone.0001326-Doyle1" class="ref-tip">[110]</a>. Sheared DNA (Gene Machines Hydroshear, Ann Arbor, MI) was size selected to produce libraries with insert sizes of 2, 3, 6, 10 and 12 Kb. DNA was ligated to a high copy plasmid vector and transformed into DH10B T1r <em>E.coli</em> cells (Invitrogen, Carlsbad, CA). The fosmid library was produced from DNA fragments between 30 and 45 Kb. DNA inserts were ligated into a pCC1FOS vector packaged with MaxPlax lambda extracts and transfected into EPI300-T1r <em>E.coli</em> cells (Epicentre, Madison, WI). LB agar contained chloramphenicol and 99,840 clones were picked (QPix2 Genetix, Hampshire, UK) into 384 well plates containing LB freezing medium, incubated for 18 h, replicated and stored at −80°C.</p>
</div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3c" name="s3c" class="link-target" title="Sanger shotgun sequencing"></a>
<h3>Sanger shotgun sequencing</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec3.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>DNA was amplified from bacterial cultures by a rolling circle technology (Templiphi kit; GE Healthcare, Amersham) and Sanger sequenced on MegaBACE 4500. Clones with inserts from 6 to 20 Kb, BAC clones and clones from fosmid libraries were amplified by the Templiphi large kit. BAC clones were bidirectional dye terminator sequenced on ABI P<span class="small-caps">rism</span>® 3730.</p>
</div>

<div id="section4" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3d" name="s3d" class="link-target" title="Sequencing by synthesis (SBS)"></a>
<h3>Sequencing by synthesis (SBS)</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec4.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec4.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Pinot Noir DNA isolated as described was subjected to nebulization to generate fragments of approximately 620 bp. These were amplified as in Margulies et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Margulies1" class="ref-tip">[14]</a> and sequenced on the Genome Sequencer 20 (Roche Applied Sciences, Indianapolis, IN). The standard protocols for 454 Sequencing using the Genome Sequencer 20 system call for the generation of a library of tagged single stranded DNA molecules (see Margulies et al <a href="#pone.0001326-Margulies1" class="ref-tip">[14]</a> for details). This single stranded library is then tested for optimal sequencing parameter through generation of sequencing beads by emulsion PCR with dilutions of the single stranded library. This titration step determined that three microlites of a single stranded library were used to generate 23 million beads. The standard GS20 pyrosequencing profile uses a sequencial flow of each nucleotide in a repeating pattern of TACG. This pattern is repeated for 42 cycles as per the standard protocol and generates 100bp of sequence information on average. For the purposes of generating longer sequencing reads the sequence profile of 42 cycles of nucleotide flows was changed to 100 cycles which increased the average read length from 105 bp to 200 bp. The GS20 has standard software to recognize high quality reads and convert the signal (light) into a base call. The standard software GS20 package was used to generate the sequence files. In total, 12.5 million reads corresponding to 2,111 million Q20 bases were produced.</p>
</div>

<div id="section5" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3e" name="s3e" class="link-target" title="Primer walking"></a>
<h3>Primer walking</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec5.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec5.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Clones bridging neighboring contigs were selected for gap closure. The clones were grown in 384-well plates and sequence-specific primers were designed and used in dye terminator sequencing reactions resolved on MegaBACE 4500.</p>
</div>

<div id="section6" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3f" name="s3f" class="link-target" title="Genome assembly"></a>
<h3>Genome assembly</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec6.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec6.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>6.2 million reads for a total of 3.5 billion Q20 bases were produced by Sanger sequencing from 43 libraries (Table S1) and about 90.6% of reads were paired. Chloroplast sequences were detected and the chloroplast genome was assembled for assessing the sequence quality and insert size distribution of each library, characteristics that were used in assembly. Chloroplast forward and reverse reads validated the correctness of data tracking and the contamination level for each sequencing plate. The size of the chloroplast genome was 160,928 bp. Remarkably, the sequence was identical (without a single mismatch) to the one already published <a href="#pone.0001326-Jansen1" class="ref-tip">[100]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec6.p2" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec6.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>SBS data were essential to identify polymorphic sites and close small gaps. The amount of chloroplast and mitochondrial sequences in SBS data was 5.5 and 2.0%, respectively, <em>vs</em> 3.1 and 1.8% in Sanger sequences. Four programs developed at Myriad Genetics Inc. were organized into a pipeline for WGS assembly: (1) Sanger and SBS sequences were compared by the Match program. It produced a table of pairwise sequence overlaps with indication of the sequence orientation, offset and match score. The overlaps were accepted if they involved more than 50 bp with no more than 2% of polymorphic positions. (2) Consensus sequences were built using the Assemble program, adapted to specified levels of heterozygosity (2% or less) and large gaps (up to 500 bp). The program reads the sequence and quality data in Fasta or GDE format, considers clone sizes and performs multiple alignments, building the consensus sequence and reporting polymorphisms of the sequence. (3) Sequences were aligned with the Align program in a two-step procedure including fast search of identical segments and optimal alignment of gaps up to 7 Kb. Larger or multiple gaps may still be a problem for the alignment and leave some overlapping contigs not merged. (4) Visual comparison of two sequences was performed by the Dotmap program. The result of the assembly is a Fasta file of assembled contig sequences with quality values assigned for each position and the list of positions of polymorphisms. (5) Metacontigs were constructed as ordered and oriented groups of contigs linked with paired reads matching to non-repetitive parts of the contigs. We used also marker information to avoid building chimeric metacontigs from different LGs (see Text S1 for more details).</p>
</div>

<div id="section7" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3g" name="s3g" class="link-target" title="Genetic maps and genome integration"></a>
<h3>Genetic maps and genome integration</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec7.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec7.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Metacontigs were integrated in the 19 grape LGs based on the genetic map derived from the cross Syrah X Pinot Noir. To improve marker density, polymorphic sites identified during WGS were selected for developing 799 additional SNP-based markers (<a href="http://genomics.research.iasma.it">http://genomics.research.iasma.it</a>) using the SNPlex™ Genotyping System <a href="#pone.0001326-Tobler1" class="ref-tip">[111]</a>. DNA was prepared according to the instructions and the samples were analyzed on the ABI PRISM® 3730xl (Applied Biosystems, Foster City, CA). Data were analyzed by Gene Mapper v. 4.0 (Applied Biosystems, Foster City, CA). The genetic maps were followed a double pseudo-testcross strategy <a href="#pone.0001326-Grattapaglia1" class="ref-tip">[112]</a>. Marker phase was determined by the Phasing algorithm (<a href="http://math.berkeley.edu/dustin/tmap/">http://math.berkeley.edu/dustin/tmap/</a>; <a href="#pone.0001326-Cartwright1" class="ref-tip">[113]</a>), which provides LG assignment and ordering of loci. LG were assembled with a minimum LOD of 8.0 and a maximum distance of 35 cM. Homologous LGs of the two parents were merged in a consensus map.</p>
</div>

<div id="section8" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3h" name="s3h" class="link-target" title="Genes and gene families"></a>
<h3>Genes and gene families</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec8.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec8.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Methods used were FgenesH <a href="#pone.0001326-Solovyev1" class="ref-tip">[114]</a>, homology-based FgenesH+ <a href="#pone.0001326-Solovyev1" class="ref-tip">[114]</a>, Twinscan <a href="#pone.0001326-Korf1" class="ref-tip">[101]</a>, GlimmerHMM <a href="#pone.0001326-Majoros1" class="ref-tip">[115]</a> and Tentative Consensus <a href="#pone.0001326-LindbladToh1" class="ref-tip">[94]</a> transcripts derived from 320,000 ESTs deposited in databases. Trimmed sequences were clustered using MegaBLAST <a href="#pone.0001326-Altschul1" class="ref-tip">[116]</a> and aligned using Cap3 <a href="#pone.0001326-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[117]</a>. After quality testing 28,856 TCs were retained.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec8.p2" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec8.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>BLAST searches against Uniprot and plant protein databases, annotated with GO terms, of various domain libraries were the base for gene annotations GO terms were extracted from BLAST searches against KEGG databases, KOBAS of metabolic pathways and InterproScan <a href="#pone.0001326-Mulder1" class="ref-tip">[118]</a> and clustered using their semantic similarity <a href="#pone.0001326-Lord1" class="ref-tip">[119]</a>, accuracy weight and the path from the root node of the ontology to the most detailed annotation. More than 79% of the gene models were annotated.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec8.p3" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec8.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Functional classification was based on Gene Ontology (<a href="http://www.geneontology.org">www.geneontology.org</a>) and manually controlled.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec8.p4" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec8.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>Homologs across species were established using a BLAST search against Rice, Poplar and Arabidopsis, considering sequence alignment coverage, best multi directional BLAST hits, sequence identity and protein domains. Sets of clusters reflected different levels of similarity among species as well as unique and putative species-specific genes. For the analysis of specific gene families, methodological variations were introduced as reported in text.</p>
</div>

<div id="section9" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3i" name="s3i" class="link-target" title="Genome duplication"></a>
<h3>Genome duplication</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec9.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec9.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Genes with similarities to TEs were removed and paralogs identified as in Li et al. <a href="#pone.0001326-Li1" class="ref-tip">[120]</a>. Age distributions were build as described by Maere et al <a href="#pone.0001326-Maere1" class="ref-tip">[105]</a>. Duplicated segments were analyzed with i-ADHoRe <a href="#pone.0001326-Simillion2" class="ref-tip">[102]</a>, based on the following parameters: gap size of 40 genes, Q value of 0.9, probability cut off of 0.001, and a minimum of 3 homologs to define a duplicated segment.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec9.p2" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec9.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Phylogenetic trees for duplicated genes (so-called anchors) in duplicated segments were based on pairs of grape paralogs representing the reciprocal best hits with aligned length of &gt;150 amino acids and considering comparisons with proteins from <em>Physcomitrella patens</em>, used as outgroup, and the best Arabidopsis homolog. Proteins were aligned with CLUSTALW and only unambiguously aligned regions were considered. Tree construction used seqbot, protdist, neighbour and consense from the PHYLIP package <a href="#pone.0001326-Felsenstein1" class="ref-tip">[121]</a> with 1000 replicates. Only topologies with over 70% bootstrap support were considered. For each paralog, if the topology was (Grape1, Grape2) Arabidopsis, it was concluded that the paralog was duplicated after the split of grape and Arabidopsis.</p>
</div>

<div id="section10" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3j" name="s3j" class="link-target" title="Repetitive elements"></a>
<h3>Repetitive elements</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec10.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec10.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Based on 10.7X coverage, a DNA segment was defined unique when associated to 15 or less matches. The threshold was selected as the middle point between two Poisson distributions, with 10X and 20X the expected coverages corresponding to unique and duplicated segments, respectively. For dispersed repetitive sequences, an iterative procedure was developed. Each segment was searched against all sequences, starting with the repeat presenting the highest number of matches. At each iteration, the program identified repeats with decreasing similarity to the original seed repeat, and the complete set of copies of a particular repeat cluster was obtained. These DNA segments were masked and the remaining sequences were searched for the next repeat with the highest number of matches. Members of each of the identified repeat types were translated and compared using BLAST program. The similarity scores were used in a UPGMA-like clusterization. Short tandem repeat (microsatellite) motifs were identified by a specifically designed program considering their number above a threshold. This was selected based on the occurrence of the motif in the genome so that the number of segments with units exceeding the threshold would be less than 1.</p>
</div>

<div id="section11" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3k" name="s3k" class="link-target" title="Non-coding RNAs"></a>
<h3>Non-coding RNAs</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec11.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec11.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Methods used for miRNA detection and individuation are cited in the caption of Figure S5. Methods and reference papers for tRNA, snRNA and snoRNA are cited in the text. Ribosomal RNA were defined and computed according to assembly program of Myriad Genetics Inc. (Salt Lake City, Utah).</p>
</div>

<div id="section12" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3l" name="s3l" class="link-target" title="Transcription factors"></a>
<h3>Transcription factors</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.sec12.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.sec12.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The reference information was from PlnTFDB, an integrate plant transcription factor database <a href="#pone.0001326-RianoPachon1" class="ref-tip">[63]</a> including genes from <em>A. thaliana</em> (ArabTFDB), <em>P. trichocarpa</em> (PoplarTFDB) and <em>O. sativa</em> (Rice TFDB) (available at <a href="http://plntfdb.bio.uni-potsdam.de">http://plntfdb.bio.uni-potsdam.de</a>). For each TF family, conserved domains were used as queries for searching similar sequences in the grape genome. The protein domains of identified TF were classified using the Pfam database <a href="#pone.0001326-Finn1" class="ref-tip">[122]</a>.</p>
</div>
</div>

<script type="text/javascript">
  var articleDoi = "10.1371/journal.pone.0001326";
  $.ajax({
    url: "https://collections.plos.org/wp-json/collections-api/contains",
    data: {id: articleDoi},
    context: document.body,
    timeout: 10000,
    dataType: "json",
    success: function (result, textStatus, xOptions) {
      if (result.response == "success" && result.data.collections.length > 0) {
        var articleCollectionsTemplate = _.template($('#articleCollectionsTemplate').html());
        var articleCollectionsHTML = articleCollectionsTemplate(result.data)
        $('.collections-aside-container').append(articleCollectionsHTML);
      }
    }
  });
</script>
